</div>
<p><b>Slow lorises</b> are a group of several <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a> <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">strepsirrhine</a> <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> that make up the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><b>Nycticebus</b></i>. Found in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast</a> <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and bordering areas, they range from <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">Northeast India</a> in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Sulu_Archipelago" title="Sulu Archipelago">Sulu Archipelago</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> in the east, and from <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> province in China in the north to the island of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> in the south. Although many previous classifications recognized as few as a single all-inclusive species, there are now at least eight that are considered valid: the <a href="/wiki/Sunda_slow_loris" title="Sunda slow loris">Sunda slow loris</a> (<i>N. coucang</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bengal_slow_loris" title="Bengal slow loris">Bengal slow loris</a> (<i>N. bengalensis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_slow_loris" title="Pygmy slow loris">pygmy slow loris</a> (<i>N. pygmaeus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Javan_slow_loris" title="Javan slow loris">Javan slow loris</a> (<i>N. javanicus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Philippine_slow_loris" title="Philippine slow loris">Philippine slow loris</a> (<i>N. menagensis</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangka_slow_loris" title="Bangka slow loris">Bangka slow loris</a> (<i>N. bancanus</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornean_slow_loris" title="Bornean slow loris">Bornean slow loris</a> (<i>N. borneanus</i>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kayan_River_slow_loris" title="Kayan River slow loris">Kayan River slow loris</a> (<i>N. kayan</i>). The group's closest relatives are the <a href="/wiki/Slender_loris" title="Slender loris">slender lorises</a> of southern India and Sri Lanka. Their next closest relatives are the African <a href="/wiki/Lorisidae" title="Lorisidae">lorisids</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Potto" title="Potto">pottos</a>, <a href="/wiki/False_potto" title="False potto">false pottos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Angwantibo" title="Angwantibo">angwantibos</a>. They are less closely related to the remaining <a href="/wiki/Lorisoidea" title="Lorisoidea">lorisoids</a> (the various types of <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">galago</a>), and more distantly to the <a href="/wiki/Lemur" title="Lemur">lemurs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>. Their evolutionary history is uncertain since their <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record is patchy and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clock</a> studies have given inconsistent results.</p>
<p>Slow lorises have a round head, a narrow snout, large eyes, and a variety of distinctive coloration patterns that are species-dependent. Their arms and legs are nearly equal in length, and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trunk_(anatomy)" title="Trunk (anatomy)">trunk</a> is long and flexible, allowing them to twist and extend to nearby branches. The hands and feet of slow lorises have several adaptations that give them a pincer-like grip and enable them to grasp branches for long periods of time. Slow lorises have a toxic bite, a trait rare among mammals and unique to lorisid primates. The <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxin</a> is obtained by licking a <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">gland</a> on their arm, and the <a href="/wiki/Secretion" title="Secretion">secretion</a> is activated by mixing with <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a>. Their toxic bite is a deterrent to predators, and the toxin is also applied to the fur during grooming as a form of protection for their infants. The secretion from the arm contains a chemical related to <a href="/wiki/Fel_d_1" title="Fel d 1">cat allergen</a>, but may be augmented by secondary toxins from the diet in wild individuals. Slow lorises move slowly and deliberately, making little or no noise, and when threatened, they stop moving and remain motionless. Their only documented predators—apart from humans—include snakes, <a href="/wiki/Changeable_hawk-eagle" title="Changeable hawk-eagle">changeable hawk-eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">orangutans</a>, although cats, <a href="/wiki/Civet" title="Civet">civets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sun_bear" title="Sun bear">sun bears</a> are suspected. Little is known about their social structure, but they are known to communicate by <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)#Spraying" title="Territory (animal)">scent marking</a>. Males are highly territorial. Slow lorises reproduce slowly, and the infants are initially parked on branches or carried by either parent. They are <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, eating small animals, fruit, <a href="/wiki/Gum_(botany)" title="Gum (botany)">tree gum</a>, and other vegetation.</p>
<p>Each of the slow loris species that had been identified prior to 2012 is listed as either "<a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">Endangered</a>" on the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a>. The three newest species are yet to be evaluated, but they arise from (and further reduce the ranks of) what was thought to be a single "vulnerable" species. All four of these are expected to be listed with at least the same, if not a higher-risk, conservation status. All slow lorises are threatened by the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_trade" title="Wildlife trade">wildlife trade</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_loss" title="Habitat loss">habitat loss</a>. Their habitat is rapidly disappearing and becoming <a href="/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation" title="Habitat fragmentation">fragmented</a>, making it nearly impossible for slow lorises to <a href="/wiki/Biological_dispersal" title="Biological dispersal">disperse</a> between forest fragments; unsustainable demand from the <a href="/wiki/Exotic_pet" title="Exotic pet">exotic pet</a> trade and from <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a> has been the greatest cause for their decline. Deep-rooted beliefs about the supernatural powers of slow lorises, such as their purported abilities to ward off evil spirits or to cure wounds, have popularized their use in traditional medicine. Despite local laws prohibiting trade in slow lorises and slow loris products, as well as protection from international commercial trade under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CITES_Appendix_I" title="CITES Appendix I">Appendix I</a>, slow lorises are openly sold in animal markets in Southeast Asia and smuggled to other countries, such as Japan. Due in part to the large eyes that are an adaptation to their nocturnal lifestyle, they have also been popularized as '<a href="/wiki/Cuteness#Juvenile_traits_and_cuteness" title="Cuteness">cute</a>' pets in <a href="/wiki/Viral_video" title="Viral video">viral videos</a> on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>. Slow lorises have their teeth cut or pulled out for the pet trade. They make poor pets that are difficult to care for, and often die from infection, blood loss, improper handling or inadequate nutrition.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; width: 248px; border: 0; padding: 0;"></td>

</table>
<p>Slow lorises (genus <i>Nycticebus</i>) are <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">strepsirrhine</a> <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> and are related to other living <a href="/wiki/Lorisoidea" title="Lorisoidea">lorisoids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Slender_loris" title="Slender loris">slender lorises</a> (<i>Loris</i>), <a href="/wiki/Potto" title="Potto">pottos</a> (<i>Perodicticus</i>), <a href="/wiki/False_potto" title="False potto">false pottos</a> (<i>Pseudopotto</i>), <a href="/wiki/Angwantibo" title="Angwantibo">angwantibos</a> (<i>Arctocebus</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">galagos</a> (family Galagidae), and to the <a href="/wiki/Lemur" title="Lemur">lemurs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup> They are most closely related to the slender lorises of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, followed by the angwantibos, pottos and false pottos of <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerelmanJohnsonRoosSeu.C3.A1nez2011figs._1.2C_2_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerelmanJohnsonRoosSeu.C3.A1nez2011figs._1.2C_2-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsRyanAttia2005fig._3_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsRyanAttia2005fig._3-6">[6]</a></sup> Lorisoids are thought to have evolved in Africa, where most living species occur;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsAttia2003421_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsAttia2003421-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsRyanAttia200511400_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsRyanAttia200511400-9">[9]</a></sup> later, one group may have migrated to Asia and evolved into the slender and slow lorises of today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200293.E2.80.9394_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200293.E2.80.9394-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lorises first appear in the Asian fossil record in the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a>, with records in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> around 18 million years ago (mya)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeinGinsburg1997783.2C_805.E2.80.93806_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeinGinsburg1997783.2C_805.E2.80.93806-11">[11]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> 16 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlynnMorgan2005100_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlynnMorgan2005100-12">[12]</a></sup> The Thai record is based on a single tooth that most closely resembles living slow lorises and that is tentatively classified as a species of <i>Nycticebus</i>. The species is named <a href="/wiki/%3F_Nycticebus_linglom" title="? Nycticebus linglom">? <i>Nycticebus linglom</i></a>, using <a href="/wiki/Open_nomenclature" title="Open nomenclature">open nomenclature</a> (the preceding "?" indicates the tentative nature of the assignment).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeinGinsburg1997805.E2.80.93806_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeinGinsburg1997805.E2.80.93806-13">[13]</a></sup> Several lorises are found in the <a href="/wiki/Sivalik_Hills" title="Sivalik Hills">Siwalik</a> deposits of Pakistan, dating to 16 to 8 mya, including <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nycticeboides&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nycticeboides (page does not exist)">Nycticeboides</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Microloris&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Microloris (page does not exist)">Microloris</a></i>. Most are small, but an unnamed form dating to 15–16 mya is comparable in size to the largest living slow lorises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlynnMorgan2005100.E2.80.93107_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlynnMorgan2005100.E2.80.93107-14">[14]</a></sup> Molecular clock analysis suggests that slow lorises may have started <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">evolving into distinct species</a> about 10 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerelmanJohnsonRoosSeu.C3.A1nez2011table_1.2C_fig._2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerelmanJohnsonRoosSeu.C3.A1nez2011table_1.2C_fig._2-15">[15]</a></sup> They are thought to have reached the islands of <a href="/wiki/Sundaland" title="Sundaland">Sundaland</a> when the <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Shelf" title="Sunda Shelf">Sunda Shelf</a> was exposed at times of low sea level, creating a <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridge</a> between the mainland and islands off the coast of Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves197152_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves197152-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_taxonomy">Discovery and taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery and taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>... it had the face of a bear, the hands of a monkey, [and] moved like a sloth ...</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">American zoologist <a href="/wiki/Dean_Conant_Worcester" title="Dean Conant Worcester">Dean Conant Worcester</a>, describing the Bornean slow loris in 1891.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWorcesterBourns1905683.E2.80.93684_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWorcesterBourns1905683.E2.80.93684-17">[17]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The earliest known mention of a slow loris in scientific literature is from 1770, when Dutchman Arnout Vosmaer (1720–1799) described a specimen of what we know today as <i>N. bengalensis</i> that he had received two years earlier. The French naturalist <a href="/wiki/Georges-Louis_Leclerc,_Comte_de_Buffon" title="Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon">Georges-Louis Leclerc, Comte de Buffon</a>, later questioned Vosmaer's decision to affiliate the animal with sloths, arguing that it was more closely aligned with the lorises of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Ceylon</a> (now Sri Lanka) and Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b45_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b45-18">[18]</a></sup> The word "loris" was first used in 1765 by Buffon as a close equivalent to a Dutch name, <i>loeris</i>. This <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> was later supported by the <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Baird_(physician)" title="William Baird (physician)">William Baird</a> in the 1820s, who noted that the Dutch word <i>loeris</i> signified "a clown".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b44.E2.80.9345_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b44.E2.80.9345-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1785, the Dutch physician and naturalist <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Boddaert" title="Pieter Boddaert">Pieter Boddaert</a> was the first to officially <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">describe</a> a species of slow loris using the name <i>Tardigradus coucang</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoddaert178567_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoddaert178567-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliot191321_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliot191321-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b46_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b46-22">[22]</a></sup> This species was based on the "tailless maucauco" described by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pennant" title="Thomas Pennant">Thomas Pennant</a> in 1781, which is thought to have been based on a Sunda slow loris, and on Vosmaer's description of a Bengal slow loris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPocock1939171_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPocock1939171-23">[23]</a></sup> Consequently, there has been some disagreement over the identity of <i>Tardigradus coucang</i>; currently the name is given to the Sunda slow loris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas1922_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas1922-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves197149_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves197149-25">[25]</a></sup> The next slow loris species to be described was <i>Lori bengalensis</i> (currently <i>Nycticebus bengalensis</i>), named by <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Germain_de_Lac%C3%A9p%C3%A8de" title="Bernard Germain de Lacépède">Bernard Germain de Lacépède</a> in 1800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELac.C3.A9p.C3.A8de180068_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELac.C3.A9p.C3.A8de180068-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHussonHolthuis1953213_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussonHolthuis1953213-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1812, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Geoffroy_Saint-Hilaire" title="Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire">Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire</a> named the genus <i>Nycticebus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeoffroy_Saint-Hilaire1812163_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeoffroy_Saint-Hilaire1812163-28">[28]</a></sup> naming it for its <a href="/wiki/Nocturnality" title="Nocturnality">nocturnal</a> behavior. The name derives from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">νύξ</span> <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>(nyx)</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> form of <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">νυκτός</span> (<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">nyktos</span>, "night"), and <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">κῆβος</span> (<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Ancient Greek transliteration">kêbos</span>, "monkey").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1904465_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1904465-31">[31]</a></sup> Geoffroy also named <i>Nycticebus javanicus</i> in this work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeoffroy_Saint-Hilaire1812164_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeoffroy_Saint-Hilaire1812164-32">[32]</a></sup> Later 19th-century authors also called the slow lorises <i>Nycticebus</i>, but most used the species name <i>tardigradus</i> (given by Linnaeus in 1758 in the <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturæ</i></a>) for slow lorises, until <a href="/wiki/Mammalogy" title="Mammalogy">mammalogists</a> <a href="/wiki/Witmer_Stone" title="Witmer Stone">Witmer Stone</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._G._Rehn&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="James A. G. Rehn (page does not exist)">James A. G. Rehn</a> clarified in 1902 that Linnaeus's name actually referred to a slender loris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoneRehn1902136.E2.80.93139_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoneRehn1902136.E2.80.93139-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several more species were named around 1900, including <i>Nycticebus menagensis</i> (originally <i>Lemur menagensis</i>) by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Lydekker" title="Richard Lydekker">Richard Lydekker</a> in 1893<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELydekker189324.E2.80.9325_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELydekker189324.E2.80.9325-34">[34]</a></sup> and <i>Nycticebus pygmaeus</i> by <a href="/wiki/J._Lewis_Bonhote" title="J. Lewis Bonhote">John James Lewis Bonhote</a> in 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisJaffe2007187.E2.80.93196_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisJaffe2007187.E2.80.93196-35">[35]</a></sup> However, in 1939 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reginald_Innes_Pocock" title="Reginald Innes Pocock">Reginald Innes Pocock</a> consolidated all slow lorises into a single <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, <i>N. coucang</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPocock1939165_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPocock1939165-36">[36]</a></sup> and in his influential 1953 book <i>Primates: Comparative Anatomy and Taxonomy</i>, primatologist <a href="/wiki/William_Charles_Osman_Hill" title="William Charles Osman Hill">William Charles Osman Hill</a> also followed this course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953a156.E2.80.93163_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953a156.E2.80.93163-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1971 <a href="/wiki/Colin_Groves" title="Colin Groves">Colin Groves</a> recognized the <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_slow_loris" title="Pygmy slow loris">pygmy slow loris</a> (<i>N. pygmaeus</i>) as a separate species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves197145_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves197145-38">[38]</a></sup> and divided <i>N. coucang</i> into four <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves197148.E2.80.9349_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves197148.E2.80.9349-39">[39]</a></sup> while in 2001 Groves opined there were three species (<i>N. coucang</i>, <i>N. pygmaeus</i>, and <i>N. bengalensis</i>), and that <i>N. coucang</i> had three subspecies (<i>Nycticebus coucang coucang</i>, <i>N. c. menagensis</i>, and <i>N. c. javanicus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroves200199_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroves200199-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slow_Loris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3216" data-file-width="4288" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Slow_Loris.jpg/220px-Slow_Loris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Slow_Loris.jpg/330px-Slow_Loris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Slow_Loris.jpg/440px-Slow_Loris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slow_Loris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Kayan River slow loris<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KayanSlowLoris_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-KayanSlowLoris-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS-kayan_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS-kayan-42">[42]</a></sup> (<i><a href="/wiki/Nycticebus_kayan" title="Nycticebus kayan">N. kayan</a></i>) was distinguished from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nycticebus_menagensis" title="Nycticebus menagensis">N. menagensis</a></i> in 2012.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2006, the Bornean slow loris was elevated to the species level (as <i>Nycticebus menagensis</i>) based on <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular analysis</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_sequence" title="Nucleic acid sequence">DNA sequences</a> of the <a href="/wiki/D-loop" title="D-loop">D-loop</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_b" title="Cytochrome b">cytochrome <i>b</i></a> gene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang20061198_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang20061198-43">[43]</a></sup> In 2008, Groves and Ibnu Maryanto confirmed the promotion of the fifth species, the Javan slow loris, to species status, a move that had been suggested in previous studies from 2000. They based their decision on an analysis of cranial <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> and characteristics of <a href="/wiki/Fur" title="Fur">pelage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrovesMaryanto2008120_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrovesMaryanto2008120-44">[44]</a></sup> Species differentiation was based largely on differences in morphology, such as size, fur color, and head markings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang20061188_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang20061188-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>To help clarify species and subspecies boundaries, and to establish whether morphology-based classifications were consistent with evolutionary relationships, the <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogenetic</a> relationships within the genus <i>Nycticebus</i> were investigated by Chen and colleagues using DNA sequences derived from the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial</a> markers D-loop and cytochrome <i>b</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang2006_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang2006-46">[46]</a></sup> Previous molecular analyses using <a href="/wiki/Karyotype" title="Karyotype">karyotypes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChenZhangShiLiu199347.E2.80.9353_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenZhangShiLiu199347.E2.80.9353-47">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Restriction_enzyme" title="Restriction enzyme">restriction enzymes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhangChenShi1993167.E2.80.93175_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhangChenShi1993167.E2.80.93175-48">[48]</a></sup> and DNA sequences<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangSuLanLiu199689.E2.80.9393_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangSuLanLiu199689.E2.80.9393-49">[49]</a></sup> were focused on understanding the relationships between a few species, not the phylogeny of the entire genus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang20061188_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang20061188-45">[45]</a></sup> The analyses published in 2006 by Chen and colleagues' proved inconclusive, although one test suggested that <i>N. coucang</i> and <i>N. bengalensis</i> apparently share a closer evolutionary relationship with each other than with members of their own species, possibly due to <a href="/wiki/Introgression" title="Introgression">introgressive hybridization</a> since the tested individuals of these two taxa originated from a region of <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatry</a> in southern Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang2006_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChenPanGrovesWang2006-46">[46]</a></sup> This hypothesis was corroborated by a 2007 study that compared the variations in mitochondrial DNA sequences between <i>N. bengalensis</i> and <i>N. coucang</i>, and suggested that there has been <a href="/wiki/Gene_flow" title="Gene flow">gene flow</a> between the two species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPanChenGrovesWang2007791.E2.80.93799_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPanChenGrovesWang2007791.E2.80.93799-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012, two <a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">taxonomic synonyms</a> (formerly recognized as subspecies) of <i>N. menagensis</i>—<i><a href="/wiki/Nycticebus_bancanus" title="Nycticebus bancanus">N. bancanus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nycticebus_borneanus" title="Nycticebus borneanus">N. borneanus</a></i>—were elevated to species status, and a new species—<i><a href="/wiki/Nycticebus_kayan" title="Nycticebus kayan">N. kayan</a></i>—was also distinguished from the same. Rachel Munds, Anna Nekaris and Susan Ford based these taxonomic revisions on distinguishable facial markings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMundsNekarisFord201346_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMundsNekarisFord201346-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2012BBC_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-2012BBC-52">[52]</a></sup> With that, the <i>N. menagensis</i> <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">species complex</a> that had been collectively known as the Bornean slow loris became four species: the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_slow_loris" title="Philippine slow loris">Philippine slow loris</a> (<i>N. menagensis</i>)),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS-menagensis_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS-menagensis-53">[53]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornean_slow_loris" title="Bornean slow loris">Bornean slow loris</a> (<i>N. borneanus</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS-borneanus_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS-borneanus-54">[54]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangka_slow_loris" title="Bangka slow loris">Bangka slow loris</a> (<i>N. bancanus</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS-bancanus_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS-bancanus-55">[55]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kayan_River_slow_loris" title="Kayan River slow loris">Kayan River slow loris</a> (<i>N. kayan</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITIS-kayan_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-ITIS-kayan-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Anatomy and physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunda_Slow_Loris_(Nycticebus_coucang)_skull.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2176" data-file-width="1956" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Sunda_Slow_Loris_%28Nycticebus_coucang%29_skull.jpg/220px-Sunda_Slow_Loris_%28Nycticebus_coucang%29_skull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Sunda_Slow_Loris_%28Nycticebus_coucang%29_skull.jpg/330px-Sunda_Slow_Loris_%28Nycticebus_coucang%29_skull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Sunda_Slow_Loris_%28Nycticebus_coucang%29_skull.jpg/440px-Sunda_Slow_Loris_%28Nycticebus_coucang%29_skull.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunda_Slow_Loris_(Nycticebus_coucang)_skull.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Skull of the Sunda slow loris</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slow lorises have a round head<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200780_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200780-56">[56]</a></sup> because their <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> is shorter than in other living strepsirrhine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007185_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007185-57">[57]</a></sup> Like other <a href="/wiki/Lorisidae" title="Lorisidae">lorisids</a>, its snout does not taper towards the front of the face as it does in lemurs, making the face appear less long and pointed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007186_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007186-58">[58]</a></sup> Compared to the slender lorises, the snout of the slow loris is even less pointed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200780_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200780-56">[56]</a></sup> As with other members of Lorisidae, its interorbital distance is shorter than in lemurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007185.E2.80.93186_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007185.E2.80.93186-59">[59]</a></sup> The skull has prominent <a href="/wiki/Sagittal_crest" title="Sagittal crest">crests</a> (ridges of bone).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliot191321_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliot191321-21">[21]</a></sup> A distinguishing feature of the slow loris skull is that the <a href="/wiki/Occipital_bone" title="Occipital bone">occipital bone</a> is flattened and faces backward. The <a href="/wiki/Foramen_magnum" title="Foramen magnum">foramen magnum</a> (hole through which the spinal cord enters) faces directly backward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007186.E2.80.93187_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007186.E2.80.93187-60">[60]</a></sup> The brains of slow lorises have more folds (convolutions) than the brains of galagos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007216_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007216-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3198" data-file-width="2024" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg/170px-Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg/255px-Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg/340px-Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Smit.Faces_of_Lorises.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coloration patterns around the eyes differ between the slender lorises (middle two) and the slow lorises (top and bottom).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The ears are small,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup> sparsely covered in hair, and hidden in the fur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> Similar to the slender lorises, the fur around and directly above the eyes is dark. Unlike the slender lorises, however, the white stripe that separates the eye rings broadens both on the tip of the nose and on the forehead while also fading out on the forehead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> Like other strepsirrhine primates, the nose and lip are covered by a moist skin called the <a href="/wiki/Rhinarium" title="Rhinarium">rhinarium</a> ("wet nose"), which is a sense organ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200754_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200754-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The eyes of slow lorises are forward-facing, which gives <a href="/wiki/Stereopsis" title="Stereopsis">stereo vision</a>. Their eyes are large<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliot191321_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliot191321-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009Adam_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-2009Adam-64">[64]</a></sup> and possess a reflective layer, called the <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a>, that improves low-light vision. It is possible that this layer blurs the images they see, as the reflected light may interfere with the <a href="/wiki/Ray_(optics)" title="Ray (optics)">incoming light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007457.E2.80.93458_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007457.E2.80.93458-65">[65]</a></sup> Slow lorises have <a href="/wiki/Monochromacy" title="Monochromacy">monochromatic vision</a>, meaning they see in shades of only one color. They lack the <a href="/wiki/Opsin" title="Opsin">opsin</a> gene that would allow them to detect short wavelength light, which includes the colors blue and green.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007464.E2.80.93465_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007464.E2.80.93465-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_formula" title="Dental formula">dental formula</a> of slow lorises is <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;" title="upper dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">2.1.3.3</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;" title="lower dentition: Incisors.Canines.Premolars.Molars">2.1.3.3</span></span> × 2 = 36</span>, meaning that on each side of the mouth there are two upper (maxillary) and lower (mandibular) <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">incisors</a>, one upper and lower <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine tooth</a>, three upper and lower <a href="/wiki/Premolar" title="Premolar">premolars</a>, and three upper and lower <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molars</a>, giving a total of 36 permanent teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliot191321_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliot191321-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007246_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007246-67">[67]</a></sup> As in all other <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">crown</a> strepsirrhines, their lower incisors and canine are procumbent (lie down and face outwards), forming a <a href="/wiki/Toothcomb" title="Toothcomb">toothcomb</a>, which is used for <a href="/wiki/Personal_grooming" title="Personal grooming">personal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_grooming" title="Social grooming">social grooming</a> and feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007246_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007246-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin197945.E2.80.9347_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin197945.E2.80.9347-68">[68]</a></sup> The toothcomb is kept clean by the <a href="/wiki/Sublingua" title="Sublingua">sublingua</a> or "under-tongue", a specialized structure that acts like a toothbrush to remove hair and other debris. The sublingua extends below the tip of the tongue and is tipped with <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratinized</a>, serrated points that rake between the front teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b73_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsman_Hill1953b73-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007421.E2.80.93423_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007421.E2.80.93423-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slow lorises have relatively large maxillary canine teeth, their inner (mesial) maxillary incisors are larger than the outer (distal) maxillary incisors, and they have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diastema_(dentistry)" title="Diastema (dentistry)">diastema</a> (gap) between the canine and the first premolar. The first <a href="/wiki/Mandibular_first_premolar" title="Mandibular first premolar">mandibular premolar</a> is elongated, and the last molar has three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cusp_(dentistry)" title="Cusp (dentistry)">cusps</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Crown_(tooth)" title="Crown (tooth)">crown</a>, the shortest of which is near the back. The bony <a href="/wiki/Palate" title="Palate">palate</a> (roof of the mouth) only goes as far back as the second molar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliot191321_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliot191321-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slow lorises range in weight from the Bornean slow loris at 265 grams (9.3 oz) to as much as 2,100 grams (74 oz) for the Bengal slow loris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010157_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010157-71">[71]</a></sup> Slow lorises have stout bodies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> and their tails are only stubs and hidden beneath the dense fur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> Their combined head and body lengths vary by species, but range from 18 to 38 cm (7.1 to 15.0 in) between all species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trunk_(anatomy)" title="Trunk (anatomy)">trunk</a> is longer than in other living strepsirrhines<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007303_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007303-72">[72]</a></sup> because they have 15–16 <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_vertebrae" title="Thoracic vertebrae">thoracic vertebrae</a>, compared to 12–14 in other living strepsirrhines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395-73">[73]</a></sup> This gives them greater mobility when twisting and extending towards nearby branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009Adam_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-2009Adam-64">[64]</a></sup> Their other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrae" title="Vertebrae">vertebrae</a> include seven <a href="/wiki/Cervical_vertebrae" title="Cervical vertebrae">cervical vertebrae</a>, six or seven <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_vertebrae" title="Lumbar vertebrae">lumbar vertebrae</a>, six or seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacral_vertebrae" title="Sacral vertebrae">sacral vertebrae</a>, and seven to eleven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caudal_vertebrae" title="Caudal vertebrae">caudal vertebrae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1599" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg/220px-Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg/330px-Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg/440px-Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nycticebus_pygmaeus_003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The eyes of slow lorises are large and have a reflective layer, called the <a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a>, to help them see better at night.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike galagos, which have longer legs than arms, slow lorises have arms and legs of nearly equal length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup> Their <a href="/wiki/Intermembral_index" title="Intermembral index">intermembral index</a> (ratio of arm to leg length) averages 89, indicating that their forelimbs are slightly shorter than their hind limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> As with the slender lorises, their arms are slightly longer than their body,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395-73">[73]</a></sup> but the extremities of slow lorises are more stout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slow lorises have a powerful grasp with both their hands and feet due to several specializations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup> They can tightly grasp branches with little effort because of a special muscular arrangement in their hands and feet, where the thumb diverges at nearly 180° from the rest of the fingers, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hallux" title="Hallux">hallux</a> (big toe) ranges between being perpendicular and pointing slightly backwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007348_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007348-75">[75]</a></sup> The toes have a large <a href="/wiki/Flexor_digitorum_longus_muscle" title="Flexor digitorum longus muscle">flexor muscle</a> that originates on the lower end of the <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">thigh bone</a>, which helps to impart a strong grasping ability to the hind limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForbes189634_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForbes189634-76">[76]</a></sup> The second digit of the hand is short compared to the other digits,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> while on the foot, the fourth toe is the longest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395-73">[73]</a></sup> The sturdy thumb helps to act like a clamp when digits three, four, and five grasp the opposite side of a tree branch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup> This gives their hands and feet a pincer-like appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup> The strong grip can be held for hours without losing sensation due to the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Rete_mirabile" title="Rete mirabile">rete mirabile</a> (network of capillaries), a trait shared among all lorises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200780_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200780-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007348_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons2007348-75">[75]</a></sup> Both slender and slow lorises have relatively short feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395-73">[73]</a></sup> Like nearly all lemuriforms, they have a <a href="/wiki/Grooming_claw" title="Grooming claw">grooming claw</a> on the second toe of each foot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395_73-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200794.E2.80.9395-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slow lorises have an unusually low <a href="/wiki/Basal_metabolic_rate" title="Basal metabolic rate">basal metabolic rate</a>, about 40% of the typical value for placental mammals of their size, comparable to that of <a href="/wiki/Sloth" title="Sloth">sloths</a>. Since they consume a relatively high-calorie diet that is available year-round, it has been proposed that this slow metabolism is due primarily to the need to eliminate toxic compounds from their food. For example, slow lorises can feed on <i><a href="/wiki/Gluta" title="Gluta">Gluta</a></i> bark, which may be fatal to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiensZitzmannHussein2006_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiensZitzmannHussein2006-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_diversity">Distribution and diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Distribution and diversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Slow lorises are found in South and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. Their collective range stretches from <a href="/wiki/Northeast_India" title="Northeast India">Northeast India</a> through <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>, east to the <a href="/wiki/Sulu_Archipelago" title="Sulu Archipelago">Sulu Archipelago</a> (the small, southern islands of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>), and south to the island of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>, and many small nearby islands).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199957_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199957-78">[78]</a></sup> They are found in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> (Northeastern states),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199957_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199957-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoudhury198889.E2.80.9394_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoudhury198889.E2.80.9394-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChoudhury199277.E2.80.9383_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChoudhury199277.E2.80.9383-80">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> province), <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>, Thailand, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, the Philippines, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199957_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199957-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._menagensis_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._menagensis-81">[81]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._coucang_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._coucang-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are currently eight recognized species. The pygmy slow loris (<i>N. pygmaeus</i>) occurs east of the <a href="/wiki/Mekong" title="Mekong">Mekong River</a> in Yunnan, Laos, Vietnam, and Cambodia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._pygmaeus_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._pygmaeus-83">[83]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornean_slow_loris" title="Bornean slow loris">Bornean slow loris</a> (<i>N. menagensis</i>), found on Borneo and nearby islands, including the Sulu Archipelago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._menagensis_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._menagensis-81">[81]</a></sup> and in 2012 was split into four distinct species (adding <i>N. bancanus</i>, <i>N. borneanus</i>, and <i>N. kayan</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMundsNekarisFord201346_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMundsNekarisFord201346-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Javan_slow_loris" title="Javan slow loris">Javan slow loris</a> (<i>N. javanicus</i>) is only found on the island of Java in Indonesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._javanicus_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._javanicus-84">[84]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sunda_slow_loris" title="Sunda slow loris">Sunda slow loris</a> (<i>N. coucang</i>) occurs on Sumatra and the <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>, including Singapore and southern Thailand (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Kra" title="Isthmus of Kra">Isthmus of Kra</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._coucang_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._coucang-82">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bengal_slow_loris" title="Bengal slow loris">Bengal slow loris</a> (<i>N. bengalensis</i>) has the largest distribution of all the slow lorises<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwapnaGuptaRadhakrishna200837.E2.80.9340_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwapnaGuptaRadhakrishna200837.E2.80.9340-85">[85]</a></sup> and can be found in Bangladesh, Cambodia, southern China, Northeast India, Laos, Burma, Thailand, and Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._bengalensis_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._bengalensis-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slow lorises range across <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical</a> regions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2006CITES_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-2006CITES-87">[87]</a></sup> and are found in primary and secondary <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforests</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> groves and <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010388_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010388-88">[88]</a></sup> They prefer forests with high, dense <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782_62-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2006CITES_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-2006CITES-87">[87]</a></sup> although some species have also been found in <a href="/wiki/Disturbance_(ecology)" title="Disturbance (ecology)">disturbed habitats</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Theobroma_cacao" title="Theobroma cacao">cacao</a> plantations and mixed-crop home gardens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010388_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010388-88">[88]</a></sup> Due largely to their nocturnal behavior and the subsequent difficulties in accurately quantifying abundance, data about the population size or distribution patterns of slow lorises is limited. In general, encounter rates are low; a combined analysis of several field studies involving <a href="/wiki/Transect" title="Transect">transect</a> surveys conducted in South and Southeast Asia determined encounter rates ranging from as high as 0.74 lorises per kilometer for <i>N. coucang</i> to as low as 0.05 lorises per kilometer for <i>N. pygmaeus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisNijman2007212_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisNijman2007212-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior_and_ecology">Behavior and ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Behavior and ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG"><img alt="A small 6-week-old infant clings to its mothers back as she climbs vertically through the branches" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1456" data-file-width="997" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG/170px-Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG/255px-Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG/340px-Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Captive_N._bengalensis_from_Laos_with_6-week_baby.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Infants are either parked on branches or carried by one of their parents while they forage.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Little is known about the social structure of slow lorises, but they generally spend most of the night foraging alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiens200231.E2.80.9332_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiens200231.E2.80.9332-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317-91">[91]</a></sup> Individuals sleep during the day, usually alone but occasionally with other slow lorises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiens200231.E2.80.9332_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiens200231.E2.80.9332-90">[90]</a></sup> Home ranges of adults may significantly overlap, and those of males are generally larger than those of females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiensZitzmann200335_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiensZitzmann200335-92">[92]</a></sup> In the absence of direct studies of the genus, primatologist Simon Bearder speculated that slow loris social behavior is similar to that of the potto, another nocturnal primate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBearder198722_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBearder198722-93">[93]</a></sup> Such a social system is distinguished by a lack of <a href="/wiki/Matriarchy" title="Matriarchy">matriarchy</a> and by factors that allow the slow loris to remain inconspicuous and minimize energy expenditure. Vocal exchanges and alarm calls are limited; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urine_marking" title="Urine marking">scent marking with urine</a> is the dominant form of communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBearder198722_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBearder198722-93">[93]</a></sup> Adult males are highly territorial and are aggressive towards other males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200783_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200783-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Animal_communication" title="Animal communication">Vocalizations</a> include an affiliative (friendly) call <i>krik</i>, and a louder call resembling a crow's caw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010387_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010387-95">[95]</a></sup> When disturbed, slow lorises can also produce a low buzzing hiss or growl. To make contact with other individuals, they emit a single high-pitched rising tone, and females use a high whistle when in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estrus" title="Estrus">estrus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200783_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200783-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slow_loris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1724" data-file-width="1449" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Slow_loris.jpg/220px-Slow_loris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Slow_loris.jpg/330px-Slow_loris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Slow_loris.jpg/440px-Slow_loris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slow_loris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In Indonesia, slow lorises are called <i>malu malu</i> or "shy one" because they freeze and cover their face when spotted.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slow lorises are slow and deliberate climbers, and often hold on to branches with three of their four limbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782.E2.80.9383_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782.E2.80.9383-96">[96]</a></sup> To move between trees, they carefully grip the terminal branches of the neighboring tree and pull themselves across the small gap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7">[7]</a></sup> They will also grip branches with only their hind feet, lift themselves upright, and quickly launch forward with their hands to catch prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782.E2.80.9383_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200782.E2.80.9383-96">[96]</a></sup> Due to their slow movement, all lorises, including the slow lorises, have a specially adapted mechanism for defense against <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a>. Their slow, deliberate movement hardly disturbs the vegetation and is almost completely silent. Once disturbed, they immediately stop moving and remain motionless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman200384_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman200384-97">[97]</a></sup> In Indonesia, slow lorises are called <i>malu malu</i> or "shy one" because they freeze and cover their face when spotted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010393_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010393-98">[98]</a></sup> If cornered, they may adopt a defensive posture by curling up and lunging at the predator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESussman200384_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESussman200384-97">[97]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Acehnese_language" title="Acehnese language">Acehnese</a> name, <i>buah angin</i> ("wind monkey"), refers to their ability to "fleetingly but silently escape".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010383_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010383-99">[99]</a></sup> Little is known about the predation of slow lorises. Documented predators include snakes, the <a href="/wiki/Changeable_hawk-eagle" title="Changeable hawk-eagle">changeable hawk-eagle</a> (<i>Nisaetus cirrhatus</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHageyFryFitch-Snyder2007254_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHageyFryFitch-Snyder2007254-100">[100]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sumatran_orangutan" title="Sumatran orangutan">Sumatran orangutans</a> (<i>Pongo abelii</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUtamiVan_Hooff1997_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUtamiVan_Hooff1997-101">[101]</a></sup> Other potential predators include cats, <a href="/wiki/Sun_bear" title="Sun bear">sun bears</a> (<i>Ursus malayanus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Binturong" title="Binturong">binturongs</a> (<i>Arctictis binturong</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Civet" title="Civet">civets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlterman1995422.E2.80.93423_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlterman1995422.E2.80.93423-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slow lorises produce a <a href="/wiki/Secretion" title="Secretion">secretion</a> from their brachial <a href="/wiki/Gland" title="Gland">gland</a> (a scent gland on the upper arm near the <a href="/wiki/Axilla" title="Axilla">axilla</a>) that is licked and mixed with their saliva. In tests, three predators—binturongs, <a href="/wiki/Clouded_leopard" title="Clouded leopard">clouded leopards</a> (<i>Neofelis nebulosa</i>), and sun bears—retreated or showed other signs of displeasure when presented with cotton swabs anointed with a mixture of the toxic secretion and the saliva, whereas the toxic secretion alone generated mild interest. Before stashing their offspring in a secure location, female slow lorises will lick their brachial glands, and then groom their young with their toothcomb, depositing the toxin on their fur. When threatened, slow lorises may also lick their brachial glands and bite their aggressors, delivering the toxin into the wounds. Slow lorises can be reluctant to release their bite, which is likely to maximize the transfer of toxins. This toxic bite is a rare trait among mammals and unique to lorisid primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlterman1995421.E2.80.93423_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlterman1995421.E2.80.93423-103">[103]</a></sup> It may also be used for defense against other slow lorises and <a href="/wiki/Parasitism" title="Parasitism">parasites</a>. According to Nekaris, this adaptation—along with vocalizations, movement, and coloration patterns similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Naja" title="Naja">true cobras</a>—may have evolved through <a href="/wiki/M%C3%BCllerian_mimicry" title="Müllerian mimicry">Müllerian mimicry</a> to protect slow lorises when they need to move across the ground due to breaks in the canopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekaris2014185_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekaris2014185-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to folklore, brachial gland secretions are generally thought to contain venom because of apparent <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylactic reactions</a> by humans following their bites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKraneItagakiNakanishiWeldon200360_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKraneItagakiNakanishiWeldon200360-105">[105]</a></sup> Slow lorises can indeed inflict painful bites. Animal dealers in Southeast Asia keep tanks of water nearby so that in case of a bite, they can submerge both their arm and the slow loris to make the animal let go.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlterman1995421.E2.80.93423_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlterman1995421.E2.80.93423-103">[103]</a></sup> The secretion from the brachial gland of captive slow lorises is similar to the allergen in cat <a href="/wiki/Dander" title="Dander">dander</a>, hence the secretions may merely elicit an allergic reaction, not toxicosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHageyFryFitch-Snyder2007265_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHageyFryFitch-Snyder2007265-106">[106]</a></sup> Loris bites cause a painful swelling, and the single case of human death reported in the scientific literature was believed to have resulted from anaphylactic shock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilde1972_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilde1972-107">[107]</a></sup> However, although the toxin is only produced when the brachial secretion and saliva are mixed, both the secretion and saliva may have unique chemical properties and may act separately. Furthermore, secondary toxins may be introduced from the consumption of wild food, augmenting the toxicity. The combined brachial secretion and saliva of recently captured wild lorises was shown to contain <a href="/wiki/Batrachotoxin" title="Batrachotoxin">batrachotoxins</a>, which were not found in slow lorises held in captivity for more than a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekaris2014184.E2.80.93185_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekaris2014184.E2.80.93185-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1043" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg/170px-Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg/255px-Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg/340px-Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nycticebus_coucang_003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slow lorises have a special network of capillaries in their hands and feet that allow them to cling to branches for hours without losing sensation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Studies suggest that slow lorises are <a href="/wiki/Polygynandry" title="Polygynandry">polygynandrous</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder201052_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder201052-109">[109]</a></sup> Infants are either parked on branches while their parents find food or else are carried by one of the parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200783_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnkel-Simons200783-94">[94]</a></sup> Due to their long <a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">gestations</a> (about six months), small litter sizes, low birth weights, long <a href="/wiki/Weaning" title="Weaning">weaning</a> times (three to six months),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010384_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010384-110">[110]</a></sup> and long gaps between births, slow loris populations have one of the slowest growth rates among mammals of similar size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201022_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201022-111">[111]</a></sup> Pygmy slow lorises are likely to give birth to twins—from 50% to 100% of births, depending on the study; in contrast, this phenomenon is rare (3% occurrence) in Bengal slow lorises. A seven-year study of captive-bred pygmy slow lorises showed a skewed sex distribution, with 1.68 males born for every 1 female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPanChenGrovesWang2007791.E2.80.93799_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPanChenGrovesWang2007791.E2.80.93799-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Breeding may be continuous throughout the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup> Copulation often occurs while suspended with the hands and feet clinging to horizontal branches for support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderSchulzeStreicher2003124_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderSchulzeStreicher2003124-112">[112]</a></sup> In captive Sunda slow lorises, mating primarily occurs between June and mid-September, with the <a href="/wiki/Estrous_cycle" title="Estrous cycle">estrus cycle</a> lasting 29 to 45 days and estrus lasting one to five days. Likewise, gestation lasts 185 to 197 days, and the young weigh between 30 and 60 grams (1.1 and 2.1 oz) at birth. Females reach <a href="/wiki/Sexual_maturity" title="Sexual maturity">sexual maturity</a> at 18 to 24 months, while males are capable of reproducing at 17 months. However, the fathers become hostile towards their male offspring after 12 to 14 months and will chase them away. In captivity, they can live 20 or more years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENowak199958_74-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENowak199958-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Slow lorises are <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, eating insects, other <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a>, small birds and reptiles, eggs, fruits, <a href="/wiki/Gum_(botany)" title="Gum (botany)">gums</a>, nectar and miscellaneous vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMenon200928_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMenon200928-114">[114]</a></sup> A 1984 study of the Sunda slow loris indicated that its diet consists of 71% fruit and gums, and 29% insects and other animal prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBearder198716_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBearder198716-115">[115]</a></sup> A more detailed study of another Sunda slow loris population in 2002 and 2003 showed different dietary proportions, consisting of 43.3% gum, 31.7% nectar, 22.5% fruit, and just 2.5% arthropods and other animal prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333-113">[113]</a></sup> The most common dietary item was nectar from flowers of the Bertram palm (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugeissona_tristis" title="Eugeissona tristis">Eugeissona tristis</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333_113-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333-113">[113]</a></sup> The Sunda slow loris eats insects that other predators avoid due to their repugnant taste or smell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Preliminary results of studies on the pygmy slow loris indicate that its diet consists primarily of gums and nectar (especially nectar from <i><a href="/wiki/Saraca_dives" title="Saraca dives">Saraca dives</a></i> flowers), and that animal prey makes up 30–40% of its diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333_113-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333-113">[113]</a></sup> However, one 2002 analysis of pygmy slow loris feces indicated that it contained 98% insect remains and just 2% plant remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nutrition_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-nutrition-116">[116]</a></sup> The pygmy slow loris often returns to the same gum feeding sites and leaves conspicuous gouges on tree trunks when inducing the flow of <a href="/wiki/Exudate" title="Exudate">exudates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333_113-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETanDrake200137_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETanDrake200137-117">[117]</a></sup> Slow lorises have been reported gouging for exudates at heights ranging from 1 m (3 ft 3 in) to as much as 12 m (39 ft); the gouging process, whereby the loris repetitively bangs its toothcomb into the hard bark, may be loud enough to be heard up to 10 m (33 ft) away. The marks remaining after gouging can be used by field workers to assess loris presence in an area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010159_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010159-118">[118]</a></sup> Captive pygmy slow lorises also make characteristic gouge marks in wooden substrates, such as branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nutrition_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-nutrition-116">[116]</a></sup> It is not known how the sympatric pygmy and Bengal slow lorises partition their feeding niches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333_113-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisBearder200728.E2.80.9333-113">[113]</a></sup> The plant gums, obtained typically from species in the family <a href="/wiki/Fabaceae" title="Fabaceae">Fabaceae</a> (peas), are high in <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, and can serve as a year-around source of food, or an emergency reserve when other preferred food items are scarce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010164_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010164-119">[119]</a></sup> Several anatomical adaptations present in slow lorises may enhance their ability to feed on exudates: a long narrow tongue to make it easier to reach gum stashed in cracks and crevices, a large <a href="/wiki/Cecum" title="Cecum">cecum</a> to help the animal digest <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">complex carbohydrates</a>, and a short <a href="/wiki/Duodenum" title="Duodenum">duodenum</a> to help quickly pass potentially toxic exudates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESwapnaRadhakrishnaGuptaKumar2010113.E2.80.93121_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwapnaRadhakrishnaGuptaKumar2010113.E2.80.93121-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010163_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisStarrCollinsWilson2010163-121">[121]</a></sup> Slow lorises can use both hands to eat while hanging upside down from a branch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317_91-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe199616.E2.80.9317-91">[91]</a></sup> They spend about 20% of their nightly activities feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiens200230_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiens200230-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Beliefs about slow lorises and their use in traditional practices are deep-rooted and go back at least 300 years, if not earlier based on oral traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010877.E2.80.93878_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010877.E2.80.93878-123">[123]</a></sup> In the late 19th and early 20th centuries, it was reported that the people from the interior of Borneo believed that slow lorises were the gatekeepers for the heavens and that each person had a personal slow loris waiting for them in the afterlife. More often, however, slow lorises are used in traditional medicine or to ward off evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882-124">[124]</a></sup> The following passage from an early textbook about primates is indicative of the superstitions associated with slow lorises:</p>
<blockquote>
