<p>Many strange powers are attributed to this animal by the natives of the countries it inhabits; there is hardly an event in life to man, woman or child, or even domestic animals, that may not be influenced for better or worse by the Slow Loris, alive or dead, or by any separate part of it, and apparently one cannot usually tell at the time, that one is under supernatural power. Thus a Malay may commit a crime he did not premeditate, and then find that an enemy had buried a particular part of a Loris under his threshold, which had, unknown to him, compelled him to act to his own disadvantage. ... [a slow loris's] life is not a happy one, for it is continually seeing ghosts; that is why it hides its face in its hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliot191330.E2.80.9331_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliot191330.E2.80.9331-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Mondulkiri_Province" title="Mondulkiri Province">Mondulkiri Province</a> of Cambodia, hunters believe that lorises can heal their own broken bones immediately after falling from a branch so that they can climb back up the tree. They also believe that slow lorises have medicinal powers because they require more than one hit with a stick to die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882-124">[124]</a></sup> In the province of <a href="/wiki/North_Sumatra" title="North Sumatra">North Sumatra</a>, the slow loris is thought to bring good luck if it is buried under a house or a road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009Adam_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-2009Adam-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882-124">[124]</a></sup> In the same province, slow loris body parts were used to place curses on enemies. In Java, it was thought that putting a piece of its skull in a water jug would make a husband more docile and submissive, just like a slow loris in the daytime. More recently, researchers have documented the belief that the consumption of loris meat was an <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiac</a> that improves "male power". The gall bladder of the Bengal slow loris has historically been used to make ink for tattoos by the village elders in <a href="/wiki/Pursat_Province" title="Pursat Province">Pursat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Koh_Kong_Province" title="Koh Kong Province">Koh Kong Provinces</a> of Cambodia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882-124">[124]</a></sup> Loris wine is a traditional Cambodian medicine supposed to alleviate the pain of childbirth, made from a mixture of loris bodies and rice wine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Black_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007Black-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation">Conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_slow_lorises" title="Conservation of slow lorises">Conservation of slow lorises</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slow_Loris_Female.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1170" data-file-width="1488" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Slow_Loris_Female.jpg/220px-Slow_Loris_Female.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Slow_Loris_Female.jpg/330px-Slow_Loris_Female.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Slow_Loris_Female.jpg/440px-Slow_Loris_Female.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slow_Loris_Female.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slow lorises are popular in the <a href="/wiki/Exotic_pet" title="Exotic pet">exotic pet</a> trade, which threatens wild populations.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The two greatest threats to slow lorises are <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_trade" title="Wildlife trade">wildlife trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderLivingstone2008_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderLivingstone2008-127">[127]</a></sup> Slow lorises have lost a significant amount of habitat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878-128">[128]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation" title="Habitat fragmentation">habitat fragmentation</a> isolating small populations and obstructing <a href="/wiki/Biological_dispersal" title="Biological dispersal">biological dispersal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._javanicus_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._javanicus-84">[84]</a></sup> However, despite the lost habitat, their decline is most closely associated with unsustainable trade, either as <a href="/wiki/Exotic_pet" title="Exotic pet">exotic pets</a> or for <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Each of the slow loris species that had been identified prior to 2012 are currently listed as either "<a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">Endangered</a>" by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) on their <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">Red List</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hance2011_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hance2011-129">[129]</a></sup> When they were all considered a single species, imprecise population data together with their regular occurrence in Southeast Asian animal markets combined to erroneously suggest that slow lorises were common. This manifested as incorrect Red List assessments of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Least_Concern" title="Least Concern">Least Concern</a>" as recently as 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCN_N._coucang_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN_N._coucang-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010383_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010383-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201017_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201017-130">[130]</a></sup> The three newest species are yet to be evaluated by the IUCN, although each were once thought to be subpopulations of the Bornean slow loris—which was evaluated as "Vulnerable" in 2008. With this division of its range and population, the Bornean slow loris and the three new species face a higher risk of extinction than before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MUNews_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-MUNews-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2007, all slow loris species have been protected from commercial international trade under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CITES_Appendix_I" title="CITES Appendix I">Appendix I</a> of <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010390_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010390-132">[132]</a></sup> Furthermore, local trade is illegal because every nation in which they occur naturally has laws protecting them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2010NatGeoNewsWatch_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-2010NatGeoNewsWatch-133">[133]</a></sup> Despite their CITES Appendix I status and local legal protection, slow lorises are still threatened by both local and international trade due to problems with enforcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010390_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisMunds2010390-132">[132]</a></sup> Surveys are needed to determine existing population densities and habitat viability for all species of slow loris. Connectivity between protected areas is important for slow lorises because they are not adapted to dispersing across the ground over large distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThornNijmanSmithNekaris2009295_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThornNijmanSmithNekaris2009295-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Populations of Bengal and Sunda slow lorises are not faring well in zoos. Of the 29 captive specimens in North American zoos in 2008, several are hybrids that cannot breed, while most are past their reproductive years. The last captive birth for these species in North America was in 2001 in San Diego. Pygmy slow lorises are doing better in North American zoos; from the late 1980s (when they were imported) to 2008, the population grew to 74 animals, with most of them born at the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Zoo" title="San Diego Zoo">San Diego Zoo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderLivingstone2008_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFitch-SnyderLivingstone2008-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife_trade">Wildlife trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wildlife trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Until the 1960s, the hunting of slow lorises was sustainable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010878-128">[128]</a></sup> but due to growing demand, decreased supply, and the subsequent increased value of the marketed wildlife, slow lorises have been <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">overexploited</a> and are in decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201017_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201017-130">[130]</a></sup> With the use of modern technology, such as battery-powered search lights, slow lorises have become easier to hunt because of their eye shine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201022_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201022-111">[111]</a></sup> Traditional medicine made from loris parts is thought to cure many diseases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882_124-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010882-124">[124]</a></sup> and the demand for this medicine from wealthy urban areas has replaced the subsistence hunting traditionally performed in poor rural areas. A survey by primatologist Anna Nekaris and colleagues (2010) showed that these belief systems were so strong that the majority of respondents expressed reluctance to consider alternatives to loris-based medicines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201017_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStarrNekarisStreicherLeung201017-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Even the best breeding facilities have great difficulty breeding lorises, and those that do often have difficulty keeping them alive. It is so easy to get access to wild-caught lorises, it is highly doubtful that a seller who claims to have captive-bred ones is telling the truth.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Primatologist Anna Nekaris, in 2009 discussing the misleading information posted on YouTube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hance2011_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hance2011-129">[129]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Slow lorises are sold locally at street markets, but are also sold internationally over the Internet and in pet stores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Sakamoto_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-2007Sakamoto-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENavarro-MontesNekarisParish2009_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENavarro-MontesNekarisParish2009-136">[136]</a></sup> They are especially popular or trendy in Japan, particularly among women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Black_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-2007Black-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Sakamoto_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-2007Sakamoto-135">[135]</a></sup> The reasons for their popularity, according to the Japan Wildlife Conservation Society, are that "they're easy to keep, they don't cry, they're small, and just very cute."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Black_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-2007Black-126">[126]</a></sup> Because of their "cuteness", videos of pet slow lorises are some of the most frequently watched animal-related <a href="/wiki/Viral_video" title="Viral video">viral videos</a> on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009Adam_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-2009Adam-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hance2011_129-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hance2011-129">[129]</a></sup> In March 2011, a newly posted video of a slow loris holding a cocktail umbrella had been viewed more than two million times, while an older video of a slow loris being tickled had been viewed more than six million times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwin2011_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwin2011-137">[137]</a></sup> According to Nekaris, these videos are misunderstood by most people who watch them, since most do not realize that it is illegal in most countries to own them as pets and that the slow lorises in the videos are only docile because that is their passive defensive reaction to threatening situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hance2011_129-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hance2011-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwin2011_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwin2011-137">[137]</a></sup> Despite frequent advertisements by pet shops in Japan, the <a href="/wiki/World_Conservation_Monitoring_Centre" title="World Conservation Monitoring Centre">World Conservation Monitoring Centre</a> reported that only a few dozen slow lorises were imported in 2006, suggesting frequent <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_smuggling" title="Wildlife smuggling">smuggling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2006CITES_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-2006CITES-87">[87]</a></sup> Slow lorises are also smuggled to China, Taiwan, Europe, Russia, the United States, and Saudi Arabia for use as pets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Black_126-3"><a href="#cite_note-2007Black-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENavarro-MontesNekarisParish2009_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENavarro-MontesNekarisParish2009-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwin2011_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sherwin2011-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg"><img alt="A small, young slow loris is gripped by its limbs while its front teeth are cut with a fingernail cutter" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1584" data-file-width="2103" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg/220px-Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg/330px-Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg/440px-Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nycticebus_tooth_removal_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slow lorises have their front teeth cut or pulled before being sold as pets, a practice that often causes infection and death.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Even within their countries of origin, slow lorises are very popular pets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010883_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010883-138">[138]</a></sup> particularly in Indonesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010877_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010877-139">[139]</a></sup> They are seen as a "living toy" for children by local people or are bought out of pity by Western tourists or expatriates. Neither local nor foreign buyers usually know anything about these primates, their endangered status, or that the trade is illegal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez200810_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez200810-140">[140]</a></sup> Furthermore, few know about their strong odor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010884_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010884-141">[141]</a></sup> or their painful bite, which may lead to anaphylaxis in some cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilde1972_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilde1972-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010884_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010884-141">[141]</a></sup> According to data compiled from monthly surveys and interviews with local traders, nearly a thousand locally sourced slow lorises exchanged hands in the <a href="/wiki/Medan" title="Medan">Medan</a> bird market in North Sumatra during the late first decade of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010883_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010883-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>International trade usually causes a high mortality rate during transit, between 30% and 90%. Slow lorises also experience many health problems due to both local and international trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Black_126-4"><a href="#cite_note-2007Black-126">[126]</a></sup> In order to give the impression that the primates are tame and appropriate pets for children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088-143">[143]</a></sup> to protect people from their potentially toxic bite,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2010NatGeoNewsWatch_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-2010NatGeoNewsWatch-133">[133]</a></sup> or to deceive buyers into thinking the animal is a baby,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Black_126-5"><a href="#cite_note-2007Black-126">[126]</a></sup> animal dealers either pull the front teeth with pliers or wire cutters or they cut them off with nail cutters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009Adam_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-2009Adam-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010883_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENekarisShepherdStarrNijman2010883-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez200810_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez200810-140">[140]</a></sup> This results in severe bleeding, which sometimes causes <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a> or death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2009Adam_64-5"><a href="#cite_note-2009Adam-64">[64]</a></sup> Dental infection is common and is fatal in 90% of cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez200810_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez200810-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088-143">[143]</a></sup> Without their teeth, the animals can no longer fend for themselves in the wild, and must remain in captivity for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez200810_140-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez200810-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088-143">[143]</a></sup> The slow lorises found in animal markets are usually underweight and malnourished, and have had their fur dyed, which complicates species identification at rescue centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENavarro-MontesNekarisParish2009_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENavarro-MontesNekarisParish2009-136">[136]</a></sup> As many as 95% of the slow lorises rescued from the markets die of dental infection or improper care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088_143-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>As part of the trade, infants are pulled prematurely from their parents, leaving them unable to remove their own urine, feces, and oily skin secretions from their fur. Slow lorises have a special network of blood vessels in their hands and feet, which makes them vulnerable to cuts when pulled from the wire cages they are kept in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2007Black_126-6"><a href="#cite_note-2007Black-126">[126]</a></sup> Slow lorises are also stress-sensitive and do not do well in captivity. Common health problems seen in pet slow lorises include undernourishment, tooth decay, diabetes, obesity, and kidney failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hance2011_129-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hance2011-129">[129]</a></sup> Infection, stress, pneumonia, and poor nutrition lead to high death rates among pet lorises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESanchez200810_140-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESanchez200810-140">[140]</a></sup> Pet owners also fail to provide proper care because they are usually asleep when the nocturnal pet is awake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hance2011_129-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hance2011-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088_143-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGreal20088-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Slow_loris&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPhillipsWalker200291_7-9"><sup><i><b>j</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPhillipsWalker2002">Phillips &amp; Walker 2002</a>, p. 91.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsAttia2003421-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeiffertSimonsAttia2003421_8-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFSeiffertSimonsAttia2003">Seiffert, Simons &amp; Attia 2003</a>, p. 421.</span></li>
